<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Catalysts">Catalysts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chromium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Catalysts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Several chromium compounds are used as <a href="/wiki/Catalyst" class="mw-redirect" title="Catalyst">catalysts</a> for processing hydrocarbons. For example, the <a href="/wiki/Phillips_catalyst" title="Phillips catalyst">Phillips catalyst</a>, prepared from chromium oxides, is used for the production of about half the world's <a href="/wiki/Polyethylene" title="Polyethylene">polyethylene</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Fe-Cr mixed oxides are employed as high-temperature catalysts for the <a href="/wiki/Water_gas_shift_reaction" class="mw-redirect" title="Water gas shift reaction">water gas shift reaction</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Copper_chromite" title="Copper chromite">Copper chromite</a> is a useful <a href="/wiki/Hydrogenation" title="Hydrogenation">hydrogenation</a> catalyst.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_use">Other use</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chromium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Other use">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Chromium&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span>  <small><i>(October 2018)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Chromium(IV)_oxide" title="Chromium(IV) oxide">Chromium(IV) oxide</a> (CrO<sub>2</sub>) is a <a href="/wiki/Magnetism" title="Magnetism">magnetic</a> compound. Its ideal shape <a href="/wiki/Anisotropy" title="Anisotropy">anisotropy</a>, which imparts high <a href="/wiki/Coercivity" title="Coercivity">coercivity</a> and remnant magnetization, made it a compound superior to the γ-Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>. Chromium(IV) oxide is used to manufacture <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_tape" title="Magnetic tape">magnetic tape</a> used in high-performance audio tape and standard <a href="/wiki/Compact_audio_cassette" class="mw-redirect" title="Compact audio cassette">audio cassettes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> Chromates can prevent corrosion of steel under wet conditions, and therefore chromates are added to drilling muds.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Chromium(III)_oxide" title="Chromium(III) oxide">Chromium(III) oxide</a> (Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>) is a metal polish known as green rouge.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Chromic_acid" title="Chromic acid">Chromic acid</a> is a powerful oxidizing agent and is a useful compound for cleaning laboratory glassware of any trace of organic compounds. It is prepared by dissolving <a href="/wiki/Potassium_dichromate" title="Potassium dichromate">potassium dichromate</a> in concentrated sulfuric acid, which is then used to wash the apparatus. <a href="/wiki/Sodium_dichromate" title="Sodium dichromate">Sodium dichromate</a> is sometimes used because of its higher solubility (50 g/L versus 200 g/L respectively). The use of dichromate cleaning solutions is now phased out due to the high toxicity and environmental concerns. Modern cleaning solutions are highly effective and chromium free.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Potassium_dichromate" title="Potassium dichromate">Potassium dichromate</a> is a chemical <a href="/wiki/Reagent" title="Reagent">reagent</a>, used as a titrating agent.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Chrome_alum" title="Chrome alum">Chrome alum</a> is <a href="/wiki/Chromium(III)_potassium_sulfate" class="mw-redirect" title="Chromium(III) potassium sulfate">Chromium(III) potassium sulfate</a> and is used as a <a href="/wiki/Mordant" title="Mordant">mordant</a> (i.e., a fixing agent) for dyes in fabric and in <a href="/wiki/Tanning_(leather)" title="Tanning (leather)">tanning</a>.</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biological_role">Biological role</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chromium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Biological role">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Chromium_deficiency" title="Chromium deficiency">Chromium deficiency</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chromium_in_glucose_metabolism" title="Chromium in glucose metabolism">Chromium in glucose metabolism</a></div>
<p>In the form trivalent chromium, Cr(III), or Cr<sup>3+</sup>, chromium was tentatively identified as an essential nutrient in the late 1950s and later accepted as a trace element for its roles in the action of <a href="/wiki/Insulin" title="Insulin">insulin</a>, a hormone critical to the metabolism and storage of carbohydrate, fat and protein.<sup id="cite_ref-ods_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ods-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> The precise mechanism of its actions in the body, however, have not been fully defined, leaving in question whether chromium is essential for healthy people.<sup id="cite_ref-ods_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ods-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> Trivalent chromium occurs in trace amounts in foods, wine and water.<sup id="cite_ref-ods_7-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ods-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> In contrast, <a href="/wiki/Hexavalent_chromium" title="Hexavalent chromium">hexavalent chromium</a> (Cr(VI) or Cr<sup>6+</sup>) is highly toxic and <a href="/wiki/Mutagen" title="Mutagen">mutagenic</a> when inhaled.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> Ingestion of chromium(VI) in water has been linked to stomach tumors, and it may also cause allergic <a href="/wiki/Contact_dermatitis" title="Contact dermatitis">contact dermatitis</a> (ACD).<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Chromium_deficiency" title="Chromium deficiency">Chromium deficiency</a>, involving a lack of Cr(III) in the body, or perhaps some complex of it, such as <a href="/wiki/Glucose_tolerance_factor" class="mw-redirect" title="Glucose tolerance factor">glucose tolerance factor</a> is controversial.<sup id="cite_ref-ods_7-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ods-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Some studies suggest that the biologically active form of chromium (III) is transported in the body via an oligopeptide called <a href="/wiki/Low-molecular-weight_chromium-binding_substance" title="Low-molecular-weight chromium-binding substance">low-molecular-weight chromium-binding substance</a> (LMWCr), which might play a role in the insulin signaling pathway.<sup id="cite_ref-pharmacological1_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pharmacological1-76">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Chromium content of common foods is generally low (1-13 micrograms per serving).<sup id="cite_ref-ods_7-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ods-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-database_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-database-77">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Chromium content of food varies widely due to differences in soil mineral content, growing season, plant <a href="/wiki/Cultivar" title="Cultivar">cultivar</a>, and contamination during processing.<sup id="cite_ref-database_77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-database-77">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> In addition, chromium (and <a href="/wiki/Nickel" title="Nickel">nickel</a>) leach into food cooked in stainless steel, with the effect largest when the cookware is new. Acidic foods such as tomato sauce which are cooked for many hours also exacerbate this effect.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dietary_recommendations">Dietary recommendations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chromium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Dietary recommendations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There is disagreement on chromium's status as an essential nutrient. Governmental departments from Australia, New Zealand, India, Japan and the United States consider chromium essential<sup id="cite_ref-AustraliaNZ_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AustraliaNZ-80">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-India_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-India-81">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Japan_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Japan-82">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-IOM-Chromium_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IOM-Chromium-83">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> while the <a href="/wiki/European_Food_Safety_Authority" title="European Food Safety Authority">European Food Safety Authority</a> (EFSA), representing the European Union, does not.<sup id="cite_ref-EFSA_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EFSA-84">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/National_Academy_of_Medicine" title="National Academy of Medicine">National Academy of Medicine</a> (NAM) updated the <a href="/wiki/Dietary_Reference_Intake#Parameters" title="Dietary Reference Intake">Estimated Average Requirements</a> (EARs) and the <a href="/wiki/Dietary_Reference_Intake#Parameters" title="Dietary Reference Intake">Recommended Dietary Allowances</a> (RDAs) for chromium in 2001. For chromium, there was not sufficient information to set EARs and RDAs, so its needs are described as estimates for <a href="/wiki/Dietary_Reference_Intake#Parameters" title="Dietary Reference Intake">Adequate Intakes</a> (AIs). The current AIs of chromium for women ages 14 through 50 is 25 μg/day, and the AIs for women ages 50 and above is 20 μg/day. The AIs for women who are pregnant are 30 μg/day, and for women who are lactating, the set AIs are 45 μg/day. The AIs for men ages 14 through 50 are 35 μg/day, and the AIs for men ages 50 and above are 30 μg/day. For children ages 1 through 13, the AIs increase with age from 0.2 μg/day up to 25 μg/day. As for safety, the NAM sets <a href="/wiki/Dietary_Reference_Intake#Parameters" title="Dietary Reference Intake">Tolerable Upper Intake Levels</a> (ULs) for vitamins and minerals when the evidence is sufficient. In the case of chromium, there is not yet enough information and hence no UL has been established. Collectively, the EARs, RDAs, AIs and ULs are the parameters for the nutrition recommendation system known as <a href="/wiki/Dietary_Reference_Intake" title="Dietary Reference Intake">Dietary Reference Intake</a> (DRI).<sup id="cite_ref-IOM-Chromium_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IOM-Chromium-83">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> Australia and New Zealand consider chromium to be an essential nutrient, with an AI of 35 μg/day for men, 25 μg/day for women, 30 μg/day for women who are pregnant, and 45 μg/day for women who are lactating. A UL has not been set due to the lack of sufficient data.<sup id="cite_ref-AustraliaNZ_80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AustraliaNZ-80">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> India considers chromium to be an essential nutrient, with an adult recommended intake of 33 μg/day.<sup id="cite_ref-India_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-India-81">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Japan also considers chromium to be an essential nutrient, with an AI of 10 μg/day for adults, including women who are pregnant or lactating. A UL has not been set.<sup id="cite_ref-Japan_82-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Japan-82">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> The EFSA of the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> however, does not consider chromium to be an essential nutrient; chromium is the only mineral for which the United States and the European Union disagree.<sup id="cite_ref-EFSA_84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EFSA-84">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>For the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States'</a> food and dietary supplement labeling purposes, the amount of the substance in a serving is expressed as a percent of the <a href="/wiki/Reference_Daily_Intake" title="Reference Daily Intake">Daily Value</a> (%DV). For chromium labeling purposes, 100% of the Daily Value was 120 μg. As of May 27, 2016, the percentage of daily value was revised to 35 μg to bring the chromium intake into a consensus with the official <a href="/wiki/Dietary_Reference_Intake" title="Dietary Reference Intake">Recommended Dietary Allowance</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FedReg_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FedReg-86">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>  The original deadline to be in compliance was July 28, 2018, but on September 29, 2017 the <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Drug_Administration" title="Food and Drug Administration">Food and Drug Administration</a> released a proposed rule that extended the deadline to January 1, 2020 for large companies and January 1, 2021 for small companies.<sup id="cite_ref-FDAdelay_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FDAdelay-87">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Food_sources">Food sources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chromium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Food sources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Food composition databases such as the those maintained by the U.S. Department of Agriculture do not contain information on the chromium content of foods.<sup id="cite_ref-USDA-Database_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USDA-Database-88">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> A wide variety of animal- and vegetable-sourced foods contain chromium.<sup id="cite_ref-IOM-Chromium_83-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IOM-Chromium-83">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> Content per serving is influenced by the chromium content of the soil in which the plants are grown and by feedstuffs fed to animals; also by processing methods, as chromium is leached into foods if processed or cooked in chromium-containing stainless steel equipment.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> One diet analysis study conducted in Mexico reported an average daily chromium intake of 30 micrograms.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> An estimated 31% of adults in the United States consume multi-vitamin/mineral dietary supplements<sup id="cite_ref-Kantor2016_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kantor2016-91">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> which often contain 25 to 60 micrograms of chromium.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Supplementation">Supplementation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chromium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Supplementation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Chromium is an ingredient in <a href="/wiki/Total_parenteral_nutrition" class="mw-redirect" title="Total parenteral nutrition">total parenteral nutrition</a> (TPN) because deficiency can occur after months of intravenous feeding with chromium-free TPN. For this reason, chromium is added to TPN solutions, along with other trace minerals.<sup id="cite_ref-Stehle2016_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stehle2016-92">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> It is also in nutritional products for <a href="/wiki/Preterm_infant" class="mw-redirect" title="Preterm infant">preterm infants</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> Although the mechanism in biological roles for chromium is unclear, in the United States chromium-containing products are sold as non-prescription dietary supplements in amounts ranging from 50 to 1,000 μg. Lower amounts of chromium are also often incorporated into multi-vitamin/mineral supplements consumed by an estimated 31% of adults in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-Kantor2016_91-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kantor2016-91">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> Chemical compounds used in dietary supplements include chromium chloride, chromium citrate, <a href="/wiki/Chromium(III)_picolinate" title="Chromium(III) picolinate">chromium(III) picolinate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chromium_polynicotinate" class="mw-redirect" title="Chromium polynicotinate">chromium(III) polynicotinate</a>, and other chemical compositions.<sup id="cite_ref-ods_7-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ods-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> The benefit of supplements has not been proven.<sup id="cite_ref-ods_7-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ods-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vincent2010_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vincent2010-94">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Approved_and_disapproved_health_claims">Approved and disapproved health claims</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chromium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Approved and disapproved health claims">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In 2005, the U.S. Food and Drug Administration had approved a Qualified Health Claim for chromium picolinate with a requirement for very specific label wording: "One small study suggests that chromium picolinate may reduce the risk of insulin resistance, and therefore possibly may reduce the risk of type 2 diabetes. FDA concludes, however, that the existence of such a relationship between chromium picolinate and either insulin resistance or type 2 diabetes is highly uncertain." At the same time, in answer to other parts of the petition, the FDA rejected claims for chromium picolinate and cardiovascular disease, retinopathy or kidney disease caused be abnormally high blood sugar levels.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> In 2010, chromium(III) picolinate was approved by Health Canada to be used in dietary supplements. Approved labeling statements include: a factor in the maintenance of good health, provides support for healthy glucose metabolism, helps the body to metabolize carbohydrates and helps the body to metabolize fats.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/European_Food_Safety_Authority" title="European Food Safety Authority">European Food Safety Authority</a> (EFSA) approved claims in 2010 that chromium contributed to normal macronutrient metabolism and maintenance of normal blood glucose concentration, but rejected claims for maintenance or achievement of a normal body weight, or reduction of tiredness or fatigue.<sup id="cite_ref-EFSA2010_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EFSA2010-97">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Diabetes">Diabetes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chromium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Diabetes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Given the evidence for chromium deficiency causing problems with glucose management in the context of intravenous nutrition products formulated without chromium,<sup id="cite_ref-Stehle2016_92-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stehle2016-92">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> research interest turned to whether chromium supplementation for people who have type 2 diabetes but are not chromium deficient could benefit. Looking at the results from four meta-analyses, one reported a statistically significant decrease in fasting <a href="/wiki/Plasma_glucose" class="mw-redirect" title="Plasma glucose">plasma glucose</a> levels (FPG) and a non-significant trend in lower <a href="/wiki/Glycated_hemoglobin" title="Glycated hemoglobin">hemoglobin A1C</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Mauro_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mauro-98">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> A second reported the same,<sup id="cite_ref-Abdoll2013_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abdoll2013-99">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> a third reported significant decreases for both measures,<sup id="cite_ref-Suk2014_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Suk2014-100">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> while a fourth reported no benefit for either.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> A review published in 2016 listed 53 <a href="/wiki/Randomized_clinical_trial" class="mw-redirect" title="Randomized clinical trial">randomized clinical trials</a> that were included in one or more of six <a href="/wiki/Meta-analysis" title="Meta-analysis">meta-analyses</a>. It concluded that whereas there may be modest decreases in FPG and/or HbA1C that achieve statistical significance in some of these meta-analyses, few of the trials achieved decreases large enough to be expected to be relevant to clinical outcome.<sup id="cite_ref-Costello2016_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Costello2016-102">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Weight_management">Weight management</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chromium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Weight management">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Two <a href="/wiki/Systematic_review" title="Systematic review">systematic reviews</a> looked at chromium supplements as a mean of managing body weight in overweight and obese people. One, limited to <a href="/wiki/Chromium_picolinate" class="mw-redirect" title="Chromium picolinate">chromium picolinate</a>, a popular supplement ingredient, reported a statistically significant -1.1&#160;kg (2.4&#160;lb) weight loss in trials longer than 12 weeks.<sup id="cite_ref-Tian2013_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tian2013-103">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> The other included all chromium compounds and reported a statistically significant -0.50&#160;kg (1.1&#160;lb) weight change.<sup id="cite_ref-Onakpoya2013_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Onakpoya2013-104">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> Change in percent body fat did not reach statistical significance. Authors of both reviews considered the clinical relevance of this modest weight loss as uncertain/unreliable.<sup id="cite_ref-Tian2013_103-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tian2013-103">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Onakpoya2013_104-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Onakpoya2013-104">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/European_Food_Safety_Authority" title="European Food Safety Authority">European Food Safety Authority</a> reviewed the literature and concluded that there was insufficient evidence to support a claim.<sup id="cite_ref-EFSA2010_97-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EFSA2010-97">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Athletic_performance">Athletic performance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chromium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Athletic performance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Chromium is promoted as a sports performance dietary supplement, based on the theory that it potentiated insulin activity, with anticipated results of increased muscle mass, and faster recovery of glycogen storage during post-exercise recovery.<sup id="cite_ref-Vincent2010_94-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vincent2010-94">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vinvent2003_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vinvent2003-106">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> A review of clinical trials reported that chromium supplementation did not improve exercise performance or increase muscle strength.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> The International Olympic Committee reviewed dietary supplements for high-performance athletes in 2018 and concluded there was no need to increase chromium intake for athletes, nor support for claims of losing body fat.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Precautions">Precautions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chromium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Precautions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Chromium_toxicity" title="Chromium toxicity">Chromium toxicity</a></div>
<p>Water-insoluble chromium(III) compounds and chromium metal are not considered a health hazard, while the toxicity and carcinogenic properties of chromium(VI) have been known for a long time.<sup id="cite_ref-Barceloux_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barceloux-109">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>  Because of the specific transport mechanisms, only limited amounts of chromium(III) enter the cells. Several <i>in vitro</i> studies indicated that high concentrations of chromium(III) in the cell can lead to DNA damage.<sup id="cite_ref-Eastmond_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eastmond-110">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> Acute oral toxicity ranges between 1.5 and 3.3&#160;mg/kg.<sup id="cite_ref-Katz_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Katz-111">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> A 2008 review suggested that moderate uptake of chromium(III) through dietary supplements poses no genetic-toxic risk.<sup id="cite_ref-Eastmond_110-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eastmond-110">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> In the US, the <a href="/wiki/Occupational_Safety_and_Health_Administration" title="Occupational Safety and Health Administration">Occupational Safety and Health Administration</a> (OSHA) has designated a <a href="/wiki/Permissible_exposure_limit" title="Permissible exposure limit">permissible exposure limit</a> (PEL) in the workplace as a time-weighted average (TWA) of 1&#160;mg/m<sup>3</sup>. The <a href="/wiki/National_Institute_for_Occupational_Safety_and_Health" title="National Institute for Occupational Safety and Health">National Institute for Occupational Safety and Health</a> (NIOSH) has set a <a href="/wiki/Recommended_exposure_limit" title="Recommended exposure limit">recommended exposure limit</a> (REL) of 0.5&#160;mg/m<sup>3</sup>, time-weighted average. The <a href="/wiki/IDLH" class="mw-redirect" title="IDLH">IDLH</a> (immediately dangerous to life and health) value is 250&#160;mg/m<sup>3</sup>.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Chromium.28VI.29_toxicity"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Chromium(VI)_toxicity">Chromium(VI) toxicity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chromium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Chromium(VI) toxicity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The acute <a href="/wiki/Mouth" title="Mouth">oral</a> <a href="/wiki/Toxicity" title="Toxicity">toxicity</a> for <a href="/wiki/Hexavalent_chromium" title="Hexavalent chromium">chromium(VI)</a> ranges between 50 and 150&#160;mg/kg.<sup id="cite_ref-Katz_111-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Katz-111">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> In the body, chromium(VI) is reduced by several mechanisms to chromium(III) already in the blood before it enters the cells. The chromium(III) is excreted from the body, whereas the chromate ion is transferred into the cell by a transport mechanism, by which also <a href="/wiki/Sulfate" title="Sulfate">sulfate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phosphate" title="Phosphate">phosphate</a> ions enter the cell. The acute toxicity of chromium(VI) is due to its strong <a href="/wiki/Oxidation" class="mw-redirect" title="Oxidation">oxidational</a> properties. After it reaches the blood stream, it damages the kidneys, the liver and blood cells through oxidation reactions. <a href="/wiki/Hemolysis" title="Hemolysis">Hemolysis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Renal" class="mw-redirect" title="Renal">renal</a>, and liver failure result. Aggressive dialysis can be therapeutic.<sup id="cite_ref-Dayan_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dayan-113">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Carcinogenity" class="mw-redirect" title="Carcinogenity">carcinogenity</a> of chromate dust has been known for a long time, and in 1890 the first publication described the elevated cancer risk of workers in a chromate dye company.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Langard_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Langard-115">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> Three mechanisms have been proposed to describe the <a href="/wiki/Genotoxicity" title="Genotoxicity">genotoxicity</a> of chromium(VI). The first mechanism includes highly reactive <a href="/wiki/Hydroxyl_radical" title="Hydroxyl radical">hydroxyl radicals</a> and other reactive radicals which are by products of the reduction of chromium(VI) to chromium(III). The second process includes the direct binding of chromium(V), produced by reduction in the cell, and chromium(IV) compounds to the <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a>. The last mechanism attributed the genotoxicity to the binding to the DNA of the end product of the chromium(III) reduction.<sup id="cite_ref-Cohen_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cohen-116">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Chromium salts (chromates) are also the cause of <a href="/wiki/Allergic_reaction" class="mw-redirect" title="Allergic reaction">allergic reactions</a> in some people. Chromates are often used to manufacture, amongst other things, leather products, paints, cement, mortar and anti-corrosives. Contact with products containing chromates can lead to allergic <a href="/wiki/Contact_dermatitis" title="Contact dermatitis">contact dermatitis</a> and irritant dermatitis, resulting in ulceration of the skin, sometimes referred to as "chrome ulcers". This condition is often found in workers that have been exposed to strong chromate solutions in electroplating, tanning and chrome-producing manufacturers.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_issues">Environmental issues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Chromium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Environmental issues">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Because chromium compounds were used in <a href="/wiki/Dye" title="Dye">dyes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paint" title="Paint">paints</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Leather" title="Leather">leather</a> <a href="/wiki/Tanning_(leather)" title="Tanning (leather)">tanning</a> compounds, these compounds are often found in soil and <a href="/wiki/Groundwater" title="Groundwater">groundwater</a> at abandoned industrial sites, now needing <a href="/wiki/Environmental_cleanup" class="mw-redirect" title="Environmental cleanup">environmental cleanup</a> and <a href="/wiki/Environmental_remediation" title="Environmental remediation">remediation</a>. <a href="/wiki/Primer_(paint)" title="Primer (paint)">Primer paint</a> containing hexavalent chromium is still widely used for <a href="/wiki/Aerospace" title="Aerospace">aerospace</a> and <a href="/wiki/Automobile" class="mw-redirect" title="Automobile">automobile</a> refinishing applications.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2010, the <a href="/wiki/Environmental_Working_Group" title="Environmental Working Group">Environmental Working Group</a> studied the drinking water in 35 American cities in the first nationwide study. The study found measurable hexavalent chromium in the tap water of 31 of the cities sampled, with <a href="/wiki/Norman,_Oklahoma" title="Norman, Oklahoma">Norman, Oklahoma</a>, at the top of list; 25 cities had levels that exceeded California's proposed limit.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
